CHAPTER IX

NavarÄtra or Durgotsava

From the 1st of Ä€Å›vina bright half to the 9th is celebrated the DurgÄpÅ«jÄ festival, otherwise called NavarÄtra, observed all over India in some form or other. Some of the works1 provide that Durgotsava must be celebrated in both Åšarad (Ä€Å›vina-Å›ukla) and Vasanta (in Caitra-Å›ukla). But it is the Durgotsava2 of Ä€Å›vina that is celebrated in a grand style, particularly in Bengal, Bihar and KÄmarÅ«pa.

If a person is unable to celebrate the festival3 for nine days, he should do so for three days beginning from the 7th tithi of Ä€Å›vina-Å›ukla. In fact, the Tithitattva 4 puts forward several alternative periods for DurgÄpuja as follows: (1) from the 9th tithi of the preceding dark half of PÅ«rá¹‡imÄnta Ä€Å›vina to the 9th tithi of Ä€Å›vina-Å›ukla; (2) from the first tithi of Ä€Å›vina Å›ukla to 9th; (3) from the 6th to the 9th; (4) from the 7th to the 9th; (5) from the MahÄá¹£á¹­ami to the 9th, (6) only on the MahÄá¹£á¹­ami; (7) only on the MahÄnavami. Most of these alternatives find support in the KÄlikÄ and other PurÄá¹‡as.

Durgotsava

There is a voluminous literature on Durgotsava. Every digest on vratas, tithis and pÅ«jÄ devotes considerable space to this subject. Moreover, there are special treatises on Durgotsava or NavarÄtra, such as the Durgotsavaviveka of SÅ«lapÄá¹‡i, the DurgÄpÅ«jÄ-prayogatattva (published by the Sanskrit SÄhity Parishad, 1924) of which the DurgÄrcanapaddhati of Raghunandana (published by Jivananda, vol. II) is a part, DurgÄbhakti tarangiá¹‡i of VidyÄpati,5 the NavarÄtra-pradipa of VinÄyaka alias Nanda-paá¹‡á¸ita, the Durgotsavapaddhati by Udayasimha (2nd half of 15th century A. D. ).6 Besides these, the MÄrkandeyapurÄá¹‡a (chapters 78-90 in Venk. Press ed. and 81-93 in Banerji’s) contains what is called DevÄ«mÄhÄtmya7 (or SaptaÅ›ati or Caá¹‡á¸i) in which are described the appearance of Devi from the accumulated tejas of Viá¹£á¹‡u, Åšhaá¹…kara, Agni and other gods, her getting different weapons from the several gods such as the trident from Åšiva, discus from Viá¹£á¹‡u, Vajra from Indra, her victory over and slaughter of the great demon MahisÄsura, and of the demons Caá¹‡á¸a and Muá¹‡á¸a, Åšumbha and NiÅ›umbha. The KÄlikÄpurÄá¹‡a, the BrÌ¥han-nandikeÅ›vara-purÄá¹‡a8 and the DevipurÄá¹‡a also devote considerable space to DurgÄ and her pÅ«jÄ. I have used, though not much, the English work on DurgÄ-pÅ«jÄ by Pratapachandra Ghosh published so far back as 1871. My endeavour will be to give as briefly as possible an account of all the salient features of this great religious worship and festival from the Sanskrit texts themselves.

This pÅ«jÄ is nitya as well as kÄmya ;9 it is the first because the KÄlikÄpurÄá¹‡a (63. 12â€“13) prescribes that whoever, through laziness, hypocrisy, hatred or stupidity, does not celebrate the great festival of DurgÄ has all his desires frustrated by the angry Devi; it is also kÄmya because rewards are promised when one celebrates Durgotsava ‘all should always perform the worship of Devi in order to secure incomparable eminence which confers the puruá¹£Ärthas, viz. Dharma, Artha, KÄma and Moká¹£a’ and ‘one should worship BhavÄni for pleasing her, for happiness in that year, for the destruction of evil spirits and goblins and for delight’ (Tithitattva p. 65). The original purposes and objects of this festival were high and noble as stated in the DevipurÄá¹‡a 10 “This is a great and holy vrata conferring great siddhis, vanquishing all enemies, conferring benefits on all people, especially in great floods; this should be performed by brÄhmaá¹‡as for solemn sacrifices and by ká¹£atriyas for the protection of the people, by vaiÅ›yas for cattle wealth, by Å›Å«dras desirous of sons and happiness, by women for blessed wifehood and by rich men who hanker for more wealth; this was performed by Åšhaá¹…kara and others”. But in course of time, the objects were brought down to the ordinary level as stated in the DevimÄhÄtmya 11 ‘by listening with devotion to my greatness in the great annual pÅ«jÄ performed in autumn, a man becomes free from all troubles and becomes endowed with wealth and agricultural produce by my favour’. The exaggerated importance of this DurgÄ-pÅ«jÄ will be clear from one verse of the Bhaviá¹£ya ‘rites like Agnihotra, solemn sacrifices described in the Vedas and completed with daká¹£iá¹‡Ä are not equal even to one hundred-thousandth part of the worship of Caá¹‡á¸ika.’12

This DurgÄpÅ«jÄ is very cosmopolitan in character. It could be performed not only by persons belonging to the four vará¹‡as, but even by those who were outside the pale of the caste system, DurgÄ-pÅ«jÄ has a congregational character and is not purely religious, but has also a social element in it (such as inviting friends and treating them &c.). The Bhaviá¹£yapurana13 says ‘Devi should be worshipped (in her shrine) on Vindhya mountain, in all places, cities, houses, villages and forests by brÄhmaá¹‡as, ká¹£atriyas, kings, vaiÅ›yas, Å›Å«dras, who are full of devotion, who have taken a bath and who are cheerful and jolly, by mlecchas and other men (such as pratilomas) and by women’. In another place the Bhaviá¹£ya states14 ‘DurgÄ is worshipped by various groups of mlecchas, by all dasyus (thievish tribes or outcaste Hindus), by people from Aá¹…ga, Vaá¹…ga and Kaliá¹…ga, by kinnaras, Barbaras (non-Aryans) and Åšakas’. Later medieval writers were careful to point out that mlecchas had no adhikÄra to perform japa or homa or pÅ«jÄ with mantras through a brÄhmaá¹‡a as Å›Å«dras had, but they could only mentally resolve to offer in honour of Devi animals as offerings and wine15 also and do so.

It is stated in the Skanda and Bhaviá¹£ya 16 purÄá¹‡as that the worship of Caá¹‡á¸ikÄ in autumn (AÅ›vina) is of a threefold character; viz. sÄttviki, rÄjasi and tÄmasÄ±; the sÄttviki worship is effected by japa, by offering naivedya in which there is no meat; and japa consists in reciting the texts of the purÄá¹‡as in which the greatness of Devi is described; rÄjasi worship is effected by offering a bali and with naivedya which contains flesh; that is tÄmasi worship which is effected by the offerings of wine and meat and from which japa is absent and which is without mantras; this last is approved among KirÄtas (i.e. forest tribes and the like). Raghunandana adds 17 in his PrÄyascittatattva that the offering of wine in DurgÄpÅ«jÄ relates to ages other than the Kali age (i.e. other than the present age).

It has been stated above (pp.110-111, n. 280) that the principal gods are deemed to go to sleep for four months on different tithis from the pratipad (1st) of Ä€á¹£Äá¸ha-Å›ukla to the 15th. Therein DurgÄ is said to go to sleep on the 8th of the bright half of Ä€á¹£Äá¸ha. Therefore, in Ä€Å›vina she would be asleep. Hence the texts provide for the rousing (bodhana) of Devi from sleep. As usual there are conflicting views. The Tithitattva 18 notices the views of some that if the Devi is to be worshipped as having 18 arms she was to be roused on the 9th of the dark half preceding AÅ›vina bright half, but that if she was to be worshipped as possessing ten arms then the bodhana was to be on the 6th (of the bright half of AÅ›vina). Raghunandana does not accept this and holds that the bodhana of DevÄ« with ten arms was to be either on the 9th (of the preceding dark half) or on the 6th (of the bright half of Ä€Å›vina). If the performer begins on the 9th of the dark half the saá¹…kalpa will be as noted below.19 He may, however, begin on the first tithi of AÅ›vina bright half and then the bodhana will be on the 6th of the bright half. If he begins on the pratipad the saá¹…kalpa will be as noted below.20 Then he should recite á¹šg. VII. 16. 11 ‘God 21 Draviá¹‡odas specially desires your ladle filled (with offering); may you pour out (Soma) or fill (the vessel with Soma); the god then immediately comes downâ€™. Then he should establish a ghaá¹­a* (a jar with a peculiar shape, the central portion bulging out, the neck narrow and the brim being broad and open), fill it with water, place twigs of mango and other sacred trees thereon and worship DurgÄ with the 16 or 5 upacÄras. Then he should offer materials such as sandal-wood paste, myrobalan for purifying the hair and also a comb.22 On the 2nd tithi he should offer a silken ribbon for keeping the hair in position, on the third alaktaka (red lac) dye for reddening the feet, vermilion for putting it on the head, a mirror for observing the face; on the 4th tithi he should offer madhuparka (to the goddess), a silver piece having the shape of a tilaka (for the forehead) and collyrium as decoration for the eyes; on the 5th tithi he should offer sandalwood paste, unguents and ornaments according to his means.

If he starts DurgÄpÅ«jÄ-utsava from the 6th tithi either conjoint with Jyeá¹£á¹­hÄ-naká¹£atra or without it he should go in the morning near a bilva tree, should utter the saá¹…kalpa noted below,23 repeat á¹šg. VII. 16. 11, establish a ghaá¹­a, should worship the bilva tree as DurgÄ. If he has already started the utsava on pratipad, then he should go near a bilva tree in the evening conjoint with Jyeá¹£á¹­hÄ or without it (and even if á¹£aá¹£á¹­hi has expired before evening, then in the evening without á¹£aá¹£á¹­hi) and rouse DevÄ« with the mantra 24 â€˜for the destruction of RÄvaá¹‡a and in order to favour RÄma, BrahmÄ, O goddess, in former times roused you at an unusual time; therefore, I also awaken you in the evening of the 6th tithi of AÅ›vina.’ After thus awakening Devi he should invite the bilva tree with the words “O bilva tree! born on the top of ÅšriÅ›aila and the abode of Laká¹£mi, I have to take you; come, you are to be worshipped as DurgÄ.’ Then he should, after taking each of the substances mentioned below and accompanying each with a mantra, place them on the bilva tree and make it an auspicious dwelling place for DurgÄ. The substances for making a holy 25 residence for Devi are; earth or clay, sandalwood paste, pebbles, coriander, dÅ«rvÄ grass, flowers, fruits, curds, ghee, svastika, vermilion, conch-shell, collyrium, rocanÄ (yellow pigment), mustard seeds, gold, silver, copper, whisk, mirror, lamp, _praÅ›astipÄtra (a broad plate). Then he should repair to the pandal erected for DurgÄpÅ«jÄ, perform Äcamana, should bind together with AparÄjita creeper twigs or leaves of nine plants 26 so as to form one bundle or sheaf, viz. plantain, pomegranate, coriander, turmeric, Arum Indicum, Arum colocacia, bilva, aÅ›oka and jayanti. Each of these substances is accompanied by an appropriate separate mantra.

He should offer worship to the bundle of nine plants and to the image (of DurgÄ) and make the Devi abide in the bilva tree with sandalwood (and other substances mentioned above). The rousing (bodhana) of the Devi and the invitation to the bilva tree are separate and are done with separate mantras (Tithitattva pp. 75-76). The two may be done at the same time if 6th tithi exists in the evening; but if it does not exist in the evening then bodhana may be done on the 6th and invitation on the 7th or bodhana may be done in the morning and invitation in the evening. The clay image of DurgÄ together with the twig of bilva is to be entered in the house and worshipped. The details of the complicated discussion are not set out here.

Then follow acts to be done on the 7th tithi, On the 7th tithi conjoint with mÅ«la-naká¹£atra or without it the performer after taking a bath approaches a bilva tree, worships it, folds his hands and says ‘O blessed bilva tree! you are always dear to Åšaá¹…kara; after taking from you a branch I shall perform DurgÄ worship; O Lord! you should not feel pain due to the cutting of a branch; O bilva! prince among trees, salutation to you!’ After saying this he should cut off from the bilva tree a branch other than one in the south-west or north-west and endowed with a couple of fruits (or without fruit) with the mantra noted below.27 Then he should take the branch, come to the place of worship (the maá¹‡á¸apa or the like ) and place it on a piá¹­ha (a seat). Then taking white mustard grains he throws them with two mantras and effects the driving away of goblins, evil spirits, rakÅ›asas, VinÄyakas that cause obstacles, takes up an offering of boiled rice and mÄá¹£a pulse and requests KÄli who is surrounded by goblins to accept that bali for protection against the evil spirits and prays her to confer upon himself all his objects in return for the offering of the bali. He then establishes the bundle of (the leaves and twigs) of nine plants tied together with AparÄjitÄ creeper and the bilva branch. He worships the latter with ‘salutation to the bilva branch’ and honours CÄmuá¹‡á¸Ä on the bilva branch and the clay image with the words ’this is pÄdya (water for washing the feet), salutation to CÄmuá¹‡á¸Ä’ and addresses the bilva branch with the mantra quoted in n. 413 above. Then he utters the mantra ‘O CÄmuá¹‡á¸Ä’! move, move, make others move, enter my house quickly and go to the place of worship, svÄhÄ’. Then he establishes the ghaá¹­a, worships the nine plants and five devatÄs, establishes the image on a seat and the bundle of nine plants to its right to the accompaniment of songs and instrumental music and places the bilva branch in a copper vessel near the (image of) Devi and performs the praá¹‡apratiá¹£á¹­hÄ 28 (vivifying or endowing with life) of the image of the Devi and of Gaá¹‡eÅ›a and other deities in the manner provided in the DurgÄrcanapaddhati (p. 666) and Tithitattva (pp. 77-78). Then he should, after taking in his hand husked rice-grains and flowers, contemplate upon the form of Devi. Then the DurgÄrcanapaddhati (pp. 666-667) quotes a long but finely worded passage from the Matsya; the corresponding passage from the KÄlikÄpurÄá¹‡a may be briefly rendered as follows: 29 Devi possesses a mass of hair, her head ornament is the crescent of the moon, she has three eyes, her face resembles a lotus and (full) moon, her complexion is like molten gold, she is well formed and has charming eyes, she is endowed with blooming youth, decked with all kinds of ornaments, has charming teeth, looks keen and has full and upraised breasts, her posture is such that she is bent at three places, she has crushed the demon Mahiá¹£a, she has ten arms long and soft like lotus stalks, she holds a trident in the right hand and a sword and discus below that (trident) in order, a sharp arrow and Å›akti (missile) in the group of her arms, a shield, a fully bent bow, a noose, a goad (held in order) from above and a bell and axe should be held in the left hand from below; under her one should show a headless buffalo from which rises a demon with a sword in his hand, who is pierced in the region of the heart by the trident (of Devi), who (demon) possesses teeth set awry, whose body is dyed red with blood, whose eyes are bloodshot and distended, who is encircled by a noose of snakes, whose face is rendered fearful by his curved eyebrows, whose hair is seized by DurgÄ with the left hand holding a noose, whose mouth emits blood; one should exhibit Devi’s lion; the right foot of Devi (should be shown) as resting evenly on the lion’s back and her left foot toe (resting) a little higher on the demon.

Then the performer should put a flower on his head, take flowers and rice grains in his hand and make an invocation (ÄvÄhana) with two mantras to DurgÄ to be present in the bilva branch and establish her with the prayer to bestow long life, health and prosperity. He offers to the Devi the several upacÄras of asana, pÄdya, arghya &c. Then he should go near the sheaf of nine plants and repeat the mantra “O blessed DurgÄ! come, enter into the (sheaf of) plants; this is your place in the mortal world; I throw myself on your mercy”! So saying he should worship Devi in the ghaá¹­a (jar) with ten or five upacÄras or with flowers and sandalwood paste with separate mantras for each of the nine plants (mentioned in note 415) in the form ‘Hrim, om, kadalisthÄyai brahmÄá¹‡yai namaá¸¥’ and so on (DurgÄrcanapaddhati 30 p. 669). Then he should worship DurgÄ with pÄdya and other upacÄras31 and offer a kid or the like as an offering. The kid or he-buffalo should be dark and should be at least five years old32.

Much has been written about the sacrifice of animals in DurgÄpÅ«jÄ. Only a few points can be dealt with here. The KÄlikÄ-purÄá¹‡a enumerates the following animals as fit for sacrifice in honour of DurgÄ and Bhairava33 “birds, tortoises, crocodiles, fish, deer of nine kinds, buffalo, gavaya(Gayal), bull, goat, mongoose, boar, rhinoceros, black antelope, Å›arabha (a fabulous animal), lion, tiger, a human being and blood from one’s own (i.e. the performer’s) body”; but the females of the species specified above were not to be offered as bali and the person who did so would go to hell. This was probably a concession to placate Devi who might have a soft corner for a bali of her own sex. The bali should have no defect and should not be one whose tail or ears were cut off. Though the above list of victims that may be offered is long, usually only goats and he-buffaloes were sacrificed. It is said34 ’the Devi that resides on Vindhya is not so well pleased with the offerings of flowers, incense, and unguents and of other animals as she is pleased with offerings of rams and he-buffaloes’. The Varsa-kriya-kaumudi35 quotes “one should never offer to the great Devi a horse or elephant; if a brÄhmaá¹‡a offers a lion, a tiger or a human being (as bali) he would go to hell and even in this world he would have a short life and would be devoid of happiness and prosperity; if (a brÄhmaá¹‡a) offers his own blood, he would be guilty of suicide. If a brÄhmaá¹‡a offered wine he would lose his status as a brÄhmaá¹‡a. When wine is laid down as a necessary offering he should offer cocoanut water in a vessel of bell-metal or honey in a copper vessel.” But there were texts that contradict this. For example, it is said in the Kalikapuraá¹‡a ‘goat, he-buffalo, human being are declared to be bali, mahÄbali and atibali, respectively.’36 It has to be remembered that though an animal is killed still what is offered to the Devi is generally blood and the head of the victim. The KÄlikÄpurÄá¹‡a37 says ‘blood purified by the recitation of mantras over it and the head (of a victim) are said to be nectar; in the worship (of Devi) the adept worshipper should offer flesh rarely (if at all) except blood and the head, which (two) become nectar.” The KÄlikÄpurÄá¹‡a further states that Åšiva (DurgÄ) partakes of the head as well as the flesh of the victim, that one should offer in pÅ«jÄ the blood and head of the victim, but the wise ( devotee ) should employ flesh in edibles and in homa. The DurgÄrcanapaddhati (pp. 669-671) prescribes an elaborate ritual about killing the bali and offering its blood and head and the rites and mantras slightly differ according as the bali is a goat, a buffalo or the performer’s own blood. The38 bali is made to face the east, the sÄdhaka (devotee) faces the north and repeats certain mantras one of which is VÄj. S. 23. 17 ‘Agni was the animal to be sacrificed. They sacrificed with him, he conquered this world (the earth) on which Agni exists; that will be your world, you will conquer that world, drink this water. VÄyu (wind God) was the animal &c.; the Sun was the animal &c.’ Another mantra is Manu V.39 (=Viá¹£á¹‡udharmasÅ«tra 51.61=KÄlikÄ 71.39) viz. ‘animals were created by BrahmÄ himself for serving the purpose of sacrifice, for the well-being of sacrifice and of all; therefore killing (an animal) in a sacrifice is no killing (does not cause the sin or blame of ordinary killing)’. He should place on the head of the bali a flower with a mantra; then he sprinkles water with kuÅ›as over the bali and then worships the sword with the mantra “you are the tongue of Caá¹‡á¸ikÄ and you accomplish heaven (for the worshipper); HrÄ«m, Srim, O sword!’. The performer contemplates on the sword as described in Tithitattva (p. 81) and DurgÄrcanapaddhati (p. 670), honours it with sandalwood paste and the rest and repeats eight names of the gword, bows to it, takes it up with the words ‘Ä€m, HrÄ«m, Phaá¹­’ and with one stroke cuts off the victim facing the east ( while the performer faces the north ) or facing the north (while the performer faces the east). Then he collects blood in a vessel of clay or other material, places it before the Devi and offers it with the words ’this is the blood of the goat; Om, O Jayanti!’ and says ‘Aim, Hrim, Åšrim, O Kausiki! May she be pleased by the blood.’ Then he offers the head of the bali (goat or the like) placing on the head a lamp with a flaming wick. He then takes blood from the sword (with which the animal’s neck was cut) and makes a tilaka mark with it on his forehead with two mantras. It was supposed that the tilaka of blood would make all persons amenable to the person who had that blood mark on the forehead as the mantra quoted below expresses. When the bali is a buffalo, the mantras repeated over a goat are omitted and two others are substituted viz. ‘as you hate a horse and as you carry Caá¹‡á¸ikÄ, so kill my enemies and bring happiness, O buffalo; you are the vehicle of Yama, you possess excellent form, you who are deathless; bestow on me long life, wealth, fame, a bow to you who are a buffalo’. The fruit of offering buffalo’s blood to Devi was deemed to be her gratification and favour for a hundred years. Then he performs japa of a mantra (quoted in note )39 and also certain verses of laudation and asks for certain benefits (DurgÄrcana. p. 672)40.

The KÄlikÄpurÄá¹‡a in a long passage41 provides gradually rising periods of times for which DurgÄ becomes gratified by the offering of the blood of various animals. A few might be cited here: “DurgÄ secures satisfaction for one month by the (offering of the) blood of fishes and tortoises, and for two months by (the blood of) crocodiles; by the blood of the black antelope and of the boar Devi is pleased for twelve years and by the blood of buffalo and rhinoceros for a hundred years; by the blood of the lion, the Å›arabha and from the performer’s body Devi is gratified for a thousand years and by a human being as a bali for a thousand years.” There was a revulsion of feeling against blood sacrifices in some hearts and so even the KÄlikÄ provides42 that the offerings of kÅ«á¹£mÄá¹‡á¸a (pumpkin-gourd) and sugarcane stalks, intoxicants and Äsavas (spirits distilled from molasses, flowers or herbs) are like (animal) victims and gratification (to the Devi) is the same as by the offering of a goat. But even in these days castes deemed higher, even including brÄhmaá¹‡as, offer goats and rarely buffaloes to DurgÄ, if that is the custom of the caste or family. I understand that many families of learned brÄhmaá¹‡as such as the BhattÄcÄryas of Bhatpara in West Bengal sacrifice fruits and vegetables in lieu of animals. The AhalyÄkÄma-dhenu43 says that some Vaiá¹£á¹‡avas regard the night when victims are killed as horrible and do not eat food or even drink water on that night and that among brÄhmaá¹‡a castes the offering (bali) should be in the form of kÅ«á¹£mÄá¹‡á¸a and the like or flour images of animals or simple ghee should be offered. As to other castes also it has been declared that kÅ«á¹£mÄá¹‡á¸a, bilva fruit, sugarcane stalks may be cut into pieces.

The convenient belief from very ancient times has been that a victim offered in sacrifice to gods and pitá¹›s went to heaven. á¹šg. I. 162.2144 = VÄj. S. 23. 16 ) and Manu V. 42 say so. HemÄdri quotes verses saying that all the animals such as the buffalo that are employed for (gratifying) Devi go to heaven and those that kill them incur no sin45.

  • God may be worshipped without an image by means of the ghaá¹­a alone.

The foregoing is somewhat of a digression, as the bali is generally to be sacrificed on the 9th and as the rites of even of the 8th have not yet been described. On the MahÄá¹£á¹­ami conjoined to PÅ«rvÄá¹£Äá¸hÄ-naká¹£atra or without it, the devotee after taking a bath and sipping water, should face the east or north, sit on a seat of darbhas, perform purification of himself and the body which is made up of five elements by repeating separately the mystic letters ‘yam, ram, lam, vam’ in the manner set out on p. 673 of DurgÄrcanapaddhati; then he performs prÄá¹‡ÄyÄma, and then nyÄsa on the several parts of the body from the heart and head to the feet. He sprinkles over himself and the materials of worship water contained in a vessel of conch-shell and the like and over which he mutters eight times the mantra ‘Hrim, Om DurgÄyai namaá¸¥’, he contemplates on DurgÄ as described on p. 163 and note 418 above, places a flower on his head, mentally offers upacÄras (to Devi), again contemplates on Devi’s form and then offers the upacÄras from Äsana (seat) to praá¹‡Äma (as indicated in note 420 above) with appropriate brief mantras. Then he offers worship to several goddesses to the right side, front side and left side of Devi with brief mantras and to the MÄtrÌ¥s46 (64 are enumerated in DurgÄrcanao. pp. 676 677, or 32, or 16 or 8) according to one’s ability with five upacÄras or with sandalwood paste and flowers and to the eight mÄtrÌ¥s BrahmÄni &c. Then there is aá¹…gapÅ«jÄ (worship of Devi’s limbs) from heart and head to the feet. Then worship is offered to her weapons such as trident, sword, discus, sharp arrow, shield, fully strung bow, the noose, goad, bell; and then her lion-seat is honoured and also armour, whisk, umbrella, flag and flag-staff, drum, conch. Separate mantras are recited in connection with the pÅ«jÄ of each of these, as laid down in DurgÄrcanapaddhati pp. 678-681, in the Nirá¹‡ayasindhu pp. 179-181 (which quotes them from Viá¹£á¹‡udharmottara) and other works. They are all passed over here for saving space. Then he goes near the sheaf of nine plants and worships it with five upacÄras or with sandalwood paste and flowers offered to each separately. Then he makes an offering of mÄá¹£a pulse and boiled rice.

On the day on which there is MahÄá¹£á¹­amipÅ«jÄ the performer observes a fast. But a householder 47 who has a son should not observe a fast on the MahÄá¹£á¹­ami. He should follow one of the alternatives specified above in note 252 (naktam haviá¹£yÄnnam &c). Whatever procedure about food he may follow, he, keeping himself pure and observing the rules of conduct for vrata, should worship Devi; he should offer worship on the MahÄá¹£á¹­ami and on the 9th with balis; he should take leave (of Devi) on the 10th with Åšravaá¹‡a-naká¹£atra after performing the ÅšÄbara festival. When the last quarter of Åšravaá¹‡a (naká¹£atra) is running then he should give a send-off to Devi on the 10th tithi. The Tithitattva 48 positively asserts that a fast is to be observed on the same day on which MahÄá¹£á¹­ami-pÅ«jÄ is performed.

On this 8th tithi the performer has to honour maidens, feed them and brÄhmaá¹‡as also. He may honour a maiden or maidens from the first to the 9th tithi, but he must do so at least on 8th. The DevipurÄá¹‡a 49 says “DurgÄ is not so much gratified by homa, gifts and japa as she is gratified (becomes favourable) by honouring maidens.” And he has to give them daká¹£iá¹‡Ä also. HemÄdri (on vrata vol. I. 903-906) quotes a long passage from the Skanda-purÄá¹‡a on this subject. Skanda provides that the performer should feed maidens from the first tithi of NavarÄtra on a rising scale, viz. one maiden on the first tithi, two on the 2nd and so on up to nine on the 9th. If he feeds nine virgins every day (of the NavarÄtra) he secures land; if he feeds double the number on each succeeding day he gets prosperity. He should not honour a maiden who is one year old (or less), for, such a girl has not developed at that age liking for fruits, flowers and sandalwood paste. Therefore, he should honour maidens who are from two to ten years old, and not those that are beyond ten years. The DevipurÄá¹‡a provides that if no virgin is available, one may honour even a married girl who has had no menstruation.50 The Skanda provides that maidens from two years to ten should be honoured under the following names respectively, viz. KumÄrika (two years old), TrimÅ«rti (three years old), KalyÄá¹‡i, Rohiá¹‡Ä«, KÄli, Caá¹‡á¸ikÄ, ÅšÄmbhavi, DurgÄ, Subhadra. The Skanda provides special pÅ«jÄ mantras for each of the nine kinds of maidens and different rewards are provided for honouring each of the nine classes. These are passed over here.

The general ÄvÄhanamantra for all in the morning after the performer has taken a ceremonial bath with oil is noted below.51 In this way he is to honour virgins with bodice, clothes, sandalwood paste, flowers and aká¹£atas, with different kinds of edibles and dishes and feed them with pÄyasa (rice boiled in milk and sugar). One should not honour a maiden that has a limb more or less, that suffers from leprosy, or that has varicose veins or has a broken head or has limbs from which blood or pus oozes or has ulcers, that is blind from birth, is squint-eyed or blind of one eye or is ugly or has little hair or much hair or is diseased or is born of a slave woman. A man should honour a brÄhmaá¹‡a maiden in all acts, a ká¹£atriya maiden for victory, one belonging to untouchable castes in acts of terrible nature. After the maidens are fed, brÄhmaá¹‡as also should be fed (DurgÄrcana. pp. 681).

The Tithitattva (pp. 69-72), Nirá¹‡ayasindhu and other works provide that in the NavarÄtra the performer should hear the recitation of the Vedas (VedapÄrÄyaá¹‡a) and that beginning with the first tithi up to the 9th he should himself mutter Caá¹‡á¸ipÄá¹­ha (recitation of DevimÄhÄtmya) or engage a person to do so, reciting it once on the first, twice on 2nd tithi and so on up to nine times on 9th tithi. The DevipurÄá¹‡a provides for pÅ«jÄ, japa and homa and the mantras therefore by saying that the worship of Devi is to be performed by casting into the fire sesame and with curds, milk and ghee. The Devi mantra is either what is called the Jayantimantra52 (quoted in note ) or the mantra of nine syllables ( quoted in the note). The Dvaitanirá¹‡aya-siddhÄntasangraha states that the mantra is either one that a man’s guru has taught him, or the Vedic verse ‘JÄtavedase sunavÄma’(á¹šg. The Tithitattva quotes (pp. 69â€“71) a long passage from the VÄrÄhi-tantra about the result of reciting DevimÄhÄtmya several times. A few verses are cited for sample. â€œWhen some great danger threatens, one should recite (DevimÄhÄtmya) seven times; if repeated twelve times one secures fulfilment of one’s desires and the destruction of enmity; if repeated 14 times, an enemy would be subdued and a woman would be brought under one’s control; repeating a hundred times results in increase of one’s kingdom; if repeated a thousand times Laká¹£mi comes to a man of her own accord. Very great importance was attached in ancient and medieval times to the recitation of mantras and even modern men brought up in English schools, colleges and Universities often implicitly believe in their efficacy, even if one may not understand their meaning. The qualities of the sounds of mantras or of parts of mantras ( such as â€˜hrim’) and their potentialities, the various devices employed when using them are deemed of far greater importance than the meaning. The GÄ«tÄ (X. 25) itself puts Japa-yajÃ±a as the highest among yajÃ±as such as those of dravya (materials or money), tapoyajÃ±a (GÄ«tÄ IV. 28). Mantras are supposed to have a tremendous effect on the sub-conscious mind and it may perhaps be so if one concentrates one’s mind thereon to the exclusion of all else. A person could himself recite the SaptaÅ›atÄ« or engage a person to read it to him who was called pÄá¹­haka or vÄcaka as in T. T. p. 72, DurgÄrcana. p. 661. Some very orthodox writers went so far as to lay down that a Å›Å«dra could only listen to the MahÄbhÄrata or SaptaÅ›ati but could not read it himself. Raghunandana 56 like Kamalakara appears to have held this view.

One should stop reading at the end of a chapter and not in its middle; but if for some reason one had to stop in the middle of a chapter, when one again commenced reading or reciting SaptaÅ›ati, one should not proceed from where one left off in the middle of a chapter, but begin with the first verse of the chapter half read or recited. Provision was made that the reader engaged for reading SaptaÅ›ati should be handsomely requited for his labour by gifts of gold, silver, cows with bell-metal pails for milking. Mr Ghosh notes (p. 39) that besides the reader of DevimÄhÄtmya a separate priest is selected to repeat the mÅ«lamantra a hundred thousand times and is called JÄpaka. The Nirá¹‡ayasindhu (pp. 185â€“186) gives elaborate descriptions of Satacaá¹‡á¸i-pÄá¹­ha and Sahasracaá¹‡á¸i. In the first, ten well-conducted brÄhmaá¹‡as including the ÄcÄrya are chosen. In Sahasracaá¹‡di one hundred brÄhmaá¹‡as are to be chosen and they are to repeat SaptaÅ›ati for four days as in Satacaá¹‡á¸i-pÄá¹­ha. A thousand brÄhmaá¹‡as are to be fed and one hundred cows are to be donated. The Nirá¹‡ayasindhu notes that these two are not included in the great digests but there is popular usage to this effect.

After each pÅ«jÄ prescribed for the day, the family members, their friends and guests sit together and partake of the prasÄda (which in the houses of the well-to-do comprises select delicacies). After the pÅ«jÄ, homa is performed as indicated above; offerings are to be made in fire called ‘Balada ‘57 with the word ‘svÄhÄ’ at the end to the accompaniment of a mantra preceded by ‘om’. It appears that in the Devi and KÄlikÄ purÄá¹‡as it is provided that even on the 8th at midnight an animal such as a goat or he-buffalo should be sacrificed and that on the 9th tithi many balis were to be sacrificed. When the Sun is in the sign of Kanya (Virgin or Virgo) and in the month of Isa (Ä€Å›vina ) one should offer pÅ«jÄ on the 9th tithi of the bright half; one should observe a fast (on that tithi) and at midnight should begin pÅ«jÄ of Devi with great expenditure and paraphernalia when the naká¹£atra is MÅ«la or PurvÄá¹£Äá¸hÄ; an animal should be killed and a he-buffalo and a goat should be sacrificed’.59

A short period of time, viz. the last ghaá¹­ikÄ 60 of MahÄá¹£á¹­ami and the first ghaá¹­ika of navami, was called ‘sandhi’. During that period of two ghaá¹­ikÄs a pÅ«jÄ of Devi together with Yoginis is to be performed which is deemed to be the holiest pÅ«jÄ (Ghosh p. 120). Now the rites of the MahÄnavami have to be described. On the 9th tithi conjoined to UttarÄá¹£ÄdhÄ naká¹£atra or without it, pÅ«jÄ is to be performed on the lines of the one of MahÄá¹£á¹­ami. This day’s puja is 62 only a repetition of that on the 8th, the only difference being that a larger number of balis (goats etc.) may be sacrificed on it than on the 8th tithi. A bali is to be offered as it is obligatory to do so on the 9th63. Then the worshipper should perform homa, if able to do so. If he be unable to perform homa on both days, he should perform it on MahÄá¹£á¹­ami. The offerings in the fire are to be 108. If a person cannot perform a homa in person he should choose a brÄhmaá¹‡a to do it, while a Å›Å«dra must always get the homa performed through a brÄhmaá¹‡a. Daká¹£iá¹‡Ä (fee) to the priest for the whole of the autumnal pÅ«jÄ is to be given on the 9th tithi and Raghunandana expressly 65 says that the DurgÄ-bhakti taraá¹…gini is wrong in saying that daká¹£iá¹‡Ä is to be paid after taking leave (visarjana) of Devi. The saá¹…kalpa at the time of giving daká¹£iá¹‡a is given in the note66. If no daká¹£iá¹‡a be paid, the whole rite becomes fruitless. Then he should pray that ‘all defects in the pÅ«jÄ may be remedied, that whatever he has offered with a devout heart, whether leaves or flowers, fruits or water, whatever naivedya he has offered, may be graciously accepted by Devi and that if any mistake has occurred by his missing some letter or mantra DevÄ« should forgive all that.’ He should wash the feet of a brÄhmaá¹‡a maiden and give her sumptuous dinner and ask her to place with her hand on his head aká¹£atas and devoutly follow her a few steps. He should feed brÄhmaá¹‡as. It is the practice in many families to embrace each other and sing the praise of DurgÄ while drums are beaten. Then in a large procession they visit the houses of such relatives and neighbours as have performed the pÅ«jÄ. Then the united procession visits other houses and the parties return the visits to each other by turns and wish happiness and good luck to each other and offer sweets. RÄjanÄ«tiprakÄÅ›a (pp. 439â€“444) quotes a long passage from the Devi-purÄá¹‡a about the worship of Devi on 9th of Ä€sv. Å›u.

On the 10th tithi after bathing and sipping water he should offer worship with sixteen upacÄras or with as many as possible, should praise Devi with these words ‘Om DurgÄm ÅšivÄm’ &c. This last means that actions usually associated with the Å›abaras (i.e. aboriginal tribes like the Bhils &c.) should be indulged in (to express ecstacy of the DurgÄpÅ«jÄ) on the 10th tithi after the immersion of the image of Devi.70 The KÄlaviveka quotes Satya to the effect that leave is taken of Devi on the last pÄda of Åšravaá¹‡a naká¹£atra in the day and Å›Äbarotsava is celebrated on the 10th, and explains that what is meant is that like men of the Åšabara tribe, people wear leaves of trees, their bodies are besmeared with mire, they make incoherent prancing movements and are engaged in dances, songs and music. The KÄlikÄpurÄá¹‡a dwells on this at great length. It says: " the visarjana ( sending away) of DevÄ« should be made with festivals in the manner of Å›abaras, viz. people may make merry to their heart’s content by throwing dust and mud, with auspicious sports and revelry, with indulgence in words and songs referring to male and female organs and with words expressive of the sexual act. The Devi becomes angry with him who does not abuse another and whom others do not abuse and pronounces on him a terrible curse.” This provision that every one, how highsoever, was to join in the Å›abarotsava was probably made for emphasizing that before the Devi the highest and the lowest were of equal status and every one must join in the frolics on the 10th tithi to show that all men were equal at least one day in the year. After the immersion friends visit friends and partake of sweets. It appears that this last practice is now not in vogue.

A few words must be said with reference to certain relevant matters. How Devi was to be contemplated upon can be seen from p. 163, note 418 above. That shows that only the image of Devi with the lion and the demon Mahiá¹£Äsura was to be constructed. In the MÄrk. puraá¹‡a71 it is stated that DevÄ« strode over the great demon’s throat, struck him with her trident and with her heavy sword cut off his head and made him fall down on the ground. But in modern times the image of Devi is placed in a structure of wickerwork and there are images of Laká¹£mi and Gaá¹‡eÅ›a on the right side and of Sarasvati and Kartikeya on the left72. Raghunandana does not make much of these additional deities, though he speaks of the prÄá¹‡apratisthÄ of Gaá¹‡eÅ›a and others. On p. 146 of ‘Cave temples of India’ by Fergusson and Burgess reference is made to a tableau at Mahavalli-puram of the strife between MahisÄsura and Devi ( with eight arms), which the learned authors assign to the period 650-700 A. D. The image may be made of gold or silver, clay, metal, stone or there may only be a picture of DevÄ«. For DurgÄpÅ«jÄ it is not absolutely necessary that there should be an image. Davi may be worshipped73 in a liá¹…ga, on an altar or in a book, in her pÄdukÄs ( wooden or stone feet), in image, in a picture or a trident or sword or in water. Some of the purÄá¹‡as like Garuá¸a and Bhaviá¹£ya74 state that on the ‘7th or 8th tithi nine wooden houses or only one with four corners may be constructed, that in the midst of it a platform or altar of four cubits in length and breadth should be made on which a throne of silk, woollen cloth and deer-skin should be prepared, whereon DurgÄ ( image ) made of gold, silver, clay or wood was to be established having four or ten arms, possessing a complexion like heated gold, three eyes and the moon as a head ornament.

There is no unanimity about the arms of Devi, The VarÄhapuraá¹‡a (95.41) speaks of 20 arms of Devi and mentions the twenty weapons she carries (95.42-43). In the DevibhÄgavata V.8.44 she is said to have 18 arms. Hemadri (on vrata, vol. I. pp. 923-924) quotes verses in which Devi is said to have eight arms and ten arms respectively. VidyÄpati75 quotes passages for both ten arms and eight arms. It appears that different numbers of arms were associated with different forms and names of Devi. For example, VidyÄpati in describing the form of KÄli or CÄmuá¹‡á¸a speaks of her as having four arms. In the VirÄá¹­aparva (chap. 6) where Yudhiá¹£á¹­hira praises DurgÄ, she is described (in verse 8) as having four arms.

There are varying views about the word ’navarÄtra’. Some hold that navarÄtra means ’nine days and nights’; it signifies only the time for a vrata (i.e. it is a guá¹‡avidhi), not the name of a rite (karma), while others contend that navarÄtra is the name of a rite or vrata, which may last for eight days ( if there is tithiká¹£aya) or ten days (if there is vá¹›ddhi of a tithi from the first tithi to the 9th). The first view is held by works like the KÄlatattvavivecana (pp. 270-272), while the 2nd is held by the Nirá¹‡ayasindhu76 (p. 165), the Puruá¹£ÄrthacintÄmaá¹‡i (p. 61) and others. As the discussion is scholastic and hardly of any practical importance it is passed over here.

The principal matters in NavarÄtra or DurgÄpÅ«ja, though it may be spread over three days ( from 7th tithi) or nine days (from 1st to 9th), are four as laid down by the Liá¹…gapuraá¹‡a viz. snÄpana 77 (bathing the image), puja, the offering of a victim (bali) and homa. For want of space snÄpana has not been dwelt upon in the above. The DurgÄrcanapaddhati (p. 674). the Vrataraja (p. 340) and other digests state at length how the image of DurgÄ may be bathed with scented water, with milk, curds, ghee, paÃ±cagavya of a kapilÄ cow, sugarcane juice and what rewards are reaped by particular kinds of baths. One example is given here for a sample.78 The man who even for a day bathes Caá¹‡á¸ikÄ with paÃ±cagavya to the accompaniment of Caá¹‡á¸igÄyatri goes near Viá¹£á¹‡u. If the image is made of some material (like clay) that cannot bear a water bath, the bright sword placed near Devi’s image may be bathed or a mirror, as stated by the KÄlikÄpurÄá¹‡a. The other three out of the four principal items in DurgÄpÅ«jÄ have been dealt with above. Out of the four, pÅ«jÄ is the most important and a fast is only an aá¹…ga (ancillary matter).

Then the question arises as to when pÅ«jÄ is to be performed. Here there are varying views. The Samaya-mayÅ«kha 79 (p. 14) quoting Devi-purÄá¹‡a appears to hold that the pÅ«jÄ should be in the morning, while the Nirá¹‡ayasindhu (p. 165) holds that it should be at night. It appears from the Devi-purÄá¹‡a and KÄlikÄpurÄá¹‡a that pÅ«jÄ in the morning, mid-day and at night is intended 80 When the sun is in sign Virgo, the devotee should follow the method of ayÄcita or nakta or take food only once or subsist only on water beginning from the nandikÄ tithi of the bright half, should bathe in the morning, should rise superior to the opposites (of heat and cold, happiness and pain &c.) and should worship (Åšiva and) ÅšivÄ three times a day.’ The KÄlatattvavivecana 81 resolves this conflict by saying that morning and midday pÅ«jÄs are brief, the principal and elaborate pÅ«jÄ is at night only. There is some confusion caused by the texts laying down the naká¹£atras, tithis or both on which the different items in the DurgÄpÅ«jÄ were to be performed.

The KÄlaviveka quotes VyÄsa as follows: ‘one should awaken DevÄ« on Ä€rdrÄ naká¹£atra, make her enter (the devotee’s house) on MÅ«la, and having worshipped her on UttarÄsÄá¸hÄ should take leave of her on Åšravaá¹‡a’. 82 Most digests refer only to the tithis. It often happened that the tithi and naká¹£atra provided for an item did not tally and some rules were evolved. The first rule is: if both tithi and naká¹£atra as provided tallied then that led to very good results, as said in the LingapurÄá¹‡a ’even if there be no MÅ«lanaká¹£atra, one should enter the bilva branch in the house on the seventh; the same rule applies to other tithis, but if the naká¹£atras exist on the tithis specified there is increase of the results or rewards.’ The second rule was that tithi was superior84 and therefore even if the naká¹£atra did not exist on the tithi specified, one should follow the tithi laid down for the several items in DurgÄpÅ«jÄ. One question on which there is difference of view is about the tithi on which NavarÄtra is to be begun when pratipad is mixed with amÄvÄsyÄ or dvitiya. There are very lengthy discussions in the Nirá¹‡ayasindhu, KÄlatattvavivecana, Puruá¹£Ärtha-cintÄmaá¹‡i and other works. Some quote passages from the Devi and other purÄá¹‡as condemning the beginning of navarÄtra on pratipad mixed with amÄvÄsyÄ. Devi is made86 to say ‘in my worship pratipad which is joined to amÄvÄsyÄ should not be accepted; pratipad existing only for a muhÅ«rta (two ghaá¹­ikÄs) at sunrise and then joined to the 2nd tithi should be accepted ( for commencing NavarÄtra festival)’. On the other hand there87 are texts like the following: if one were to accept for the (beginning of the ) worship of Caá¹‡dikÄ pratipad joined to part of 2nd tithi through ignorance or through another’s advice, that leads to the death of that person’s son. The SamayamayÅ«kha says that both classes of texts are baseless or if they are authoritative they leave the matter undecided as they are opposed to each other. The decision has to be arrived at in a different manner. ‘If pratipad is mixed with amÄvÄsyÄ but does not exist the next day, then it must be accepted for starting NavarÄtra as there is no other alternative. Therefore, even a pratipad mixed with amÄvÄsyÄ has to be accepted in such a case in spite of texts to the contrary’.

If pratipad starts with sunrise and spreads over the next day for less than three muhurtas, then the full pratipad has to be accepted for beginning navarÄtra. If at first there is amÄvÄsyÄ for one muhÅ«rta, then pratipad follows and then spreads over the next day up to less than three muhurtas, then this latter, that is, pratipad mixed with dvitÄ«yÄ has to be accepted as the beginning. For want of space all possible alternatives are not dealt with here.

One important matter was not stressed above. On the first day of NavarÄtra one has to establish a kalaÅ›a or ghaá¹­a. A full kalaÅ›a has been a symbol of holiness and prosperity from early Vedic times (compare á¹šg. III. 32.15 ‘ÄpÅ«rá¹‡o asya kalaÅ›aá¸¥’). This is a rite which is performed in the Deccan, Gujarat and SaurÄá¹£tra even though the elaborate DurgÄpÅ«jÄ of Bengal finds no place there. The procedure given in DurgÄbhaktitarangiá¹‡i p. 3. Ghaá¹­asthÄpana is spoken of in DurgÄrcanapaddhati (p. 663) and has to be performed by day and not at night. One should make 88 an altar with holy clay and sow yava and wheat grains thereon with á¹šg. X. 97. 22, and establish a jar there of gold, silver, copper or clay with á¹šg. In the Deccan the main items in the NavarÄtra are the establishment of ghaá¹­a, offering a garland of flowers on every tithi up to the 9th, a strict code of conduct (such as not shaving oneself) to be observed by the performer. If on the pratipad day on which the jar is to be established there is citrÄ-naká¹£atra 90 and the astrological conjunction called Vaidhá¹›ti, it is to be done at the end of these, but if these two exist the whole of the day, then the rite should be performed in the latter part of these two. The S. M. held that these texts were baseless. According to Ghosh (p. 76), in Bengal nine jars are established in a figure (sarvatobhadra) filled with coloured powders at prescribed places and these are decorated with nine differently coloured flags. But in some parts of India only one ghaá¹­a is established.

HemÄdri (on Vrata, vol. I. p. 906 ) quotes DevipurÄá¹‡a for honouring horses91. The DurgÄpÅ«jÄ was meant for all. Kings and such men as kept horses were advised to honour horses in NavarÄtra from the 2nd tithi in conjunction with SvÄti-naká¹£atra up to 9th tithi. The DurgÄbhaktitarangiá¹‡i devotes several pages to this nirÄjana of horses from ÅšÄlihotra, Bhoja, VarÄhamihira and other authorities (pp. 56-63 and 67-69). Kings were advised to perform LohabhisÄrika rite about horses from Ä€Å›vinÅ›ukla first to MahÄnavamÄ± (H. V. I. 910-916, N. S. 178, R. N.P. 414-416). For LohÄbhihÄrika or NirÄjana rite, vide H. of Dh. Vol III. p. 230 notes 309-311 and p. 910 note 1773. Although the Ká¹›tyakalpataru (on vrata) does not speak of Durgotsava on the lines stated above, the NaiyatakÄlika section of that work (pp. 408-410) quotes a long passage from the Bhaviá¹£yapurÄá¹‡a92 which states that Mahiá¹£a was killed by Caá¹‡á¸ikÄ and therefore she should be worshipped on navami, all vará¹‡as should worship her, kings should fast on it and gifts should be made; the Devi is pleased by the offering of the devotee’s own blood and flesh and of he-buffaloes, goats and rams. It specifies the periods for which gratification is felt by the Devi by the offering of various balis and avers that all dasyus and various mleccha tribes, the Aá¹…gas, Vaá¹…gas, Kaliá¹…gas, Kinnaras, Barbaras and Åšakas worship her.

The KrÌ¥tyakalpataru on Vrata does contain a vrata called DurgÄá¹£á¹­amivrata derived from the DevipurÄá¹‡a (pp. 225-233), but it has nothing to do with the exploits of DurgÄ.

HemÄdri also (on vrata, vol. I. pp. 856-862) contains the same vrata with a few variations. That vrata is entirely different from the Durgotsava described in the KÄlikÄpurÄá¹‡a. In the first place there is no reference in it to the killing of Mahiá¹£Äsura. In the next place, that vrata is described as beginning from the 8th of ÅšrÄvaá¹‡a-Å›ukla and going on for one year in each succeeding month on the 8th of the bright half. Feeding virgins and brÄhmaá¹‡as is mentioned in it also, but in each month the food offered to Devi and to the virgins is generally different.

About the antiquity of the worship of DurgÄ, vide H. of Dh. Vol. II pp. 738â€“739. A few matters that were not and that could not be mentioned there are noted here. AmbikÄ is said to be the sister of Åšiva in Tai.93 S., but in the times of the Tai. Ä€r. X 18 Åšiva is said to be the husband of AmbikÄ or Uma. In the Vanaparva chap. 6 (where there is a long laud of DurgÄ 94 by Yudhiá¹£á¹­hira), she is described as the daughter of YaÅ›odÄ and Nanda and as the sister of VÄsudeva, is addressed as KÄli, MahÄkÄli and DurgÄ, in verses 10-11 the weapons carried by her in her hands are mentioned and in verse 15 she is said to have destroyed Mahiá¹£Äsura. When Arjuna (in Bhisma 23) offers a DurgÄstotra at the bidding of Ká¹›á¹£á¹‡a, he gives her several names, among which are KumÄri, KÄli, KapÄli, KapilÄ, BhadrakÄli, MahÄkÄli, Caá¹‡á¸i, KÄtyÄyani, KauÅ›iki, UmÄ. But as the dates of these passages in the MahÄbhÄrata are far from certain no chronological conclusion can be stated.

Literary works and coins supply some firm data about the antiquity of the DurgÄ cult. In the Raghuvaá¹Å›a chap. II a lion is said to have been appointed a guard to protect a devadÄrÅ« (pine) tree planted by PÄrvati. PÄrvati is called Gauri in Raghu. II. 26 and in KumÄrasambhava. VII. 95 and BhavÄni in KumÄrao (VII. 84), Caá¹‡á¸i in MeghadÅ«ta (I. 33). Similarly, in the KumÄrasambhava the ArdhanÄrÄ«Å›vara form of Åšiva is mentioned.95 The mÄtrÌ¥s are referred to in the same work (VII. 30, 38) and also KÄli with skulls as ornaments (ibid. VII. 39). In the MÄlati-mÄdhava (Act V) the offering of human victims to CÄmuá¹‡á¸Ä in the city of Padmavati is mentioned. In the MrÌ¥cchakaá¹­ika VI. 27 the legend of the killing of Åšumbha and NiÅ›umbha by DurgÄ occurs. If we hold that KÄlidÄsa flourished between 350-450 A. D., the above evidence makes it quite probable that the worship of DurgÄ may go back some centuries before 300 A. D. The evidence of coins lends support to this conclusion. A siá¹havÄhini goddess is seen on the reverse of the coins of Candragupta I of the Gupta 96 dynasty (about 305-325 A. D.). “On a coin of the late Kuá¹£Äá¹‡a king Kaneshko the reverse shows the figure of a goddess with a lunar crescent seated full face on a lion crouching left and the godddess appears to have a noose and sceptre.97 The lion as vehicle and the noose indicate that the goddess should be DurgÄ and not Laká¹£mi. This would take us to the first or 2nd century A. D.

Why two NavarÄtras were recognized (in Caitra and Ä€Å›vina) is a matter of conjecture. It is possible that these two pÅ«jÄs arose from the fact that spring crops and autumn crops became ready at these two times. The worship of DurgÄ was very much influenced by ÅšÄkta doctrines and practices. Ghosh in his work on ‘DurgÄpÅ«jÄ’ (p. XVI ff.) tries to establish that the Vedic idea of Dawn (Uá¹£as) spreading over the sky was changed by the Puraá¹‡as and Tantras into the terrific goddess DurgÄ. I am not prepared to accept this explanation at all, as the links between the Vedic goddess Dawn and the terrible goddess of the KÄlikÄ-purÄá¹‡a are altogether wanting and as there is a gap of many centuries between the disappearance of Uá¹£as from the ancient pantheon and popular worship and the emergence of DurgÄ. There may be some astrological connection between DurgÄ ( Virgo ) riding a lion (Leo). But this also cannot be clearly made out.

In the Indian Historical Quarterly vol. 21 (pp. 227â€“231 ) Mr. N. G. Banerjee refers to the Durgotsava-paddhati of Udaya simha which begins with the MahÄnavami and a saá¹…kalpa for victory and ends with the description of horses marching on daÅ›ami to victory and avers that this work indicates that DurgÄpÅ«jÄ was originally a military rite and was later on transformed into a religious festival. He relies upon the description in the Raghuvaá¹Å›a (IV. 24-25) how the advent of Sarad (autumn) season impelled Raghu to march on an invasion and to perform the Å›Änti rite for horses called AÅ›vanÄ«rÄjana and also on the BrÌ¥hatsamhitÄ. chap. 44 (Kern’s ed.) where occurs an elaborate description of the nirÄjana of horses, elephants and fighters on the 8th, 12th, or 15th of the bright half of Ä€Å›vina or KÄrtika. There are serious objections against accepting this theory. It is not unknown that unconnected celebrations may be joined together on the same tithi or tithis. For example, in many parts of North India the RÄmalÄ«lÄ festival for ten days is associated with NavarÄtra days. But NavarÄtra and RÄmalila are independent of each other and in some parts even of North India, only one or the other is popular and celebrated and not both. There is nothing improbable in there being two occasions for nirÄjana, one in Durgotsava and another on the occasion of a king’s march on an invasion. Besides, a work of the 15th century can hardly be relied upon as evidence for explaining the origin of a festival that was in vogue for about a thousand years at least before that work. Moreover, if it was the usage to celebrate the marching on an invasion in Ä€Å›vina with nirÄjana (lustration), that rite might have been borrowed from that in Durgotsava by analogy instead of the utsava having adopted the military usage. The KÄlikÄ-purÄá¹‡a which describes at length Durgotsava also speaks of the nirÄjana in Ä€Å›vinaÅ›ukla on the 8th and the worship of a noble handsome horse for seven days with sandalwood paste, flowers &c. and requires the observation of the horse’s movements for drawing prognostications (chap. 88.15â€“75). There is nothing to show that the details of Durgotsava described in the KÄlikÄ and other purÄá¹‡as did not exist in the 4th, 5th and 6th centuries A. D. when KÄlidÄsa and VarÄhamihira flourished and mentioned the lustration of horses. The theory of Mr. N. G. Banerjee appears to be unwarranted by the evidence so far available and cannot be accepted.
